<p>The <b>Edmonton Oilers</b> are a professional <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> team based in <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>. They are members of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Division_(NHL)" title="Pacific Division (NHL)">Pacific Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Conference_(NHL)" title="Western Conference (NHL)">Western Conference</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> (NHL).</p>
<p>The Oilers were founded on November 1, 1971, with the team playing its first season in <a href="/wiki/1972%E2%80%9373_WHA_season" title="1972–73 WHA season">1972</a>, as one of the twelve founding franchises of the major professional <a href="/wiki/World_Hockey_Association" title="World Hockey Association">World Hockey Association</a> (WHA). They were originally intended to be one of two WHA teams in Alberta (the other one being the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Broncos" title="Calgary Broncos">Calgary Broncos</a>). However, when the Broncos relocated to <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Crusaders" title="Cleveland Crusaders">Cleveland, Ohio</a>, before the WHA's first season began, the Oilers were renamed the <b>Alberta Oilers</b>. They returned to using the Edmonton Oilers name for the <a href="/wiki/1973%E2%80%9374_WHA_season" title="1973–74 WHA season">following year</a>, and have been called that ever since. The Oilers subsequently joined the NHL in <a href="/wiki/1979%E2%80%9380_NHL_season" title="1979–80 NHL season">1979</a> as one of four franchises introduced through the NHL merger with the WHA.</p>
<p>After joining the NHL, the Oilers went on to win the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> on five occasions: <a href="/wiki/1984_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1984 Stanley Cup Finals">1983–84</a>, <a href="/wiki/1985_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1985 Stanley Cup Finals">1984–85</a>, <a href="/wiki/1987_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1987 Stanley Cup Finals">1986–87</a>, <a href="/wiki/1988_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1988 Stanley Cup Finals">1987–88</a> and <a href="/wiki/1990_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1990 Stanley Cup Finals">1989–90</a>. This remains the most championships won by any team since the NHL-WHA merger and also the most won by any team that joined the league in or after 1967. Among all NHL teams, only the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a> have won more Cups since the League's 1967 expansion. For their success in the 1980s, the Oilers team of this era has been honoured with <a href="/wiki/Dynasty_(sports)#Ice_hockey" title="Dynasty (sports)">dynasty</a> status by the <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hockey Hall of Fame">Hockey Hall of Fame</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Home_arenas"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Home arenas</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Broadcasters"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Broadcasters</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Edmonton_Oilers" title="History of the Edmonton Oilers">History of the Edmonton Oilers</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WHA_years_.281972.E2.80.931979.29">WHA years (1972–1979)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: WHA years (1972–1979)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 1, 1971, the Edmonton Oilers became one of the 12 founding WHA franchises. The original team owner was <a href="/wiki/Bill_Hunter_(ice_hockey)" title="Bill Hunter (ice hockey)">Bill Hunter</a>. Hunter owned the <a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Oil_Kings_(WCHL)" title="Edmonton Oil Kings (WCHL)">Edmonton Oil Kings</a>, a junior hockey franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-whl_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whl-2">[2]</a></sup> He also founded the Canadian Major Junior Hockey League (now known as the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hockey_League" title="Western Hockey League">Western Hockey League</a> (WHL)).<sup id="cite_ref-whl_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whl-2">[2]</a></sup> Hunter's efforts to bring major professional hockey to <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a> via an expansion NHL franchise had been rebuffed by the NHL. So, he looked to the upstart WHA instead. It was Hunter who chose the "Oilers" name for the new WHA franchise. This was a name that had previously been used as a nickname for the Edmonton Oil Kings in the 1950s and 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the newly founded <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Broncos" title="Calgary Broncos">Calgary Broncos</a> folded prior to commencement of the inaugural WHA season, the Oilers were renamed the <b>Alberta Oilers</b> as it was planned to split their home games between Edmonton and <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>. Possibly for financial reasons or to allow for a less complicated return of the WHA to Calgary, though, the team ultimately played all of its home games in the Edmonton Gardens and subsequently changed its name back to the Edmonton Oilers the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> They won the first game in WHA history 7–4 over the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Nationals" title="Ottawa Nationals">Ottawa Nationals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Oilers drew fans with players such as <a href="/wiki/Defenceman_(ice_hockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Defenceman (ice hockey)">defenceman</a> and team <a href="/wiki/Captain_(hockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Captain (hockey)">captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Al_Hamilton" title="Al Hamilton">Al Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goaltender" title="Goaltender">goaltender</a> <a href="/wiki/Dave_Dryden" title="Dave Dryden">Dave Dryden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forward_(hockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Forward (hockey)">forwards</a> <a href="/wiki/Blair_MacDonald" title="Blair MacDonald">Blair MacDonald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Flett" title="Bill Flett">Bill Flett</a>. However, a relatively little-noticed move in <a href="/wiki/1976%E2%80%9377_WHA_season" title="1976–77 WHA season">1976</a> would have an important impact on the history of the franchise. That year, journeyman forward <a href="/wiki/Glen_Sather" title="Glen Sather">Glen Sather</a> was acquired by the Oilers.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It turned out to be his final season as a player. However, he was named player-coach late in the season, moving to the bench full-time after the season. Sather would be the coach or general manager of the Oilers for the next 23 years.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the Oilers' on-ice performance for most of the WHA's history was mediocre, they remained relatively well-supported and financially stable by WHA standards. In 1976, the franchise was acquired by wealthy entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pocklington" title="Peter Pocklington">Peter Pocklington</a>. The team's fortunes would improve dramatically in <a href="/wiki/1978%E2%80%9379_WHA_season" title="1978–79 WHA season">1978</a> when Pocklington acquired <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Gretzky" title="Wayne Gretzky">Wayne Gretzky</a> as an under-age player (consequently, his first year of WHA experience prevented him from being an official <a href="/wiki/1979%E2%80%9380_NHL_season" title="1979–80 NHL season">1979–80</a> NHL <a href="/wiki/Rookie" title="Rookie">rookie</a>), as well as goaltender <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Mio" title="Eddie Mio">Eddie Mio</a> and forward <a href="/wiki/Peter_Driscoll" title="Peter Driscoll">Peter Driscoll</a>, from the recently folded <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_Racers" title="Indianapolis Racers">Indianapolis Racers</a> for cash.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Gretzky's first and only WHA season, 1978–79, saw the Oilers finish first in the WHA standings, posting a league-best 48–30–2 record.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> However, Edmonton failed to win the championship, as they fell to the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Jets_(1972%E2%80%9396)" title="Winnipeg Jets (1972–96)">Winnipeg Jets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Avco_World_Trophy" title="Avco World Trophy">Avco World Trophy</a> Final. <a href="/wiki/Dave_Semenko" title="Dave Semenko">Dave Semenko</a> of the Oilers scored the last goal in WHA history in the third period of the final game, which the Oilers lost 7–3.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Oilers joined the NHL for <a href="/wiki/1979%E2%80%9380_NHL_season" title="1979–80 NHL season">1979–80</a>, along with fellow WHA teams <a href="/wiki/Hartford_Whalers" title="Hartford Whalers">Hartford Whalers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Nordiques" title="Quebec Nordiques">Quebec Nordiques</a> and the Jets following a <a href="/wiki/NHL%E2%80%93WHA_merger" title="NHL–WHA merger">merger agreement</a> between the two leagues. Of these four teams, only Edmonton has avoided relocation and renaming; the Nordiques became the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Avalanche" title="Colorado Avalanche">Colorado Avalanche</a> in 1995, the Jets became the <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Coyotes" title="Arizona Coyotes">Phoenix Coyotes</a> in 1996 and the Whalers became the <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Hurricanes" title="Carolina Hurricanes">Carolina Hurricanes</a> in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entry_into_the_NHL_.281979.E2.80.931983.29">Entry into the NHL (1979–1983)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Entry into the NHL (1979–1983)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Oilers lost most of the players from 1978–79 when the NHL held a reclamation draft of players who had bolted to the upstart league as they were allowed to protect two goaltenders and two skill players.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Originally, Gretzky was not eligible to be protected; under the rules of the time, he normally would have been placed in the <a href="/wiki/1979_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="1979 NHL Entry Draft">Entry Draft</a> pool. However, Pocklington had signed him to a 21-year personal services contract in 1979 and Pocklington used the contract to force the NHL to admit the Oilers and allow the Oilers to keep Gretzky.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Oilers were mediocre during the regular season in their first two seasons, finishing sixteenth and fourteenth respectively. However, due to the fact that 16 of the 21 NHL teams made the playoffs at the time, the Oilers were still able to get their young players experience in the playoffs (they would make the playoffs for their first thirteen years in the NHL).<sup id="cite_ref-Oilers_season_results_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oilers_season_results-14">[14]</a></sup> They won only one playoff series over this time span though, upsetting the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a> in <a href="/wiki/1980-81_NHL_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1980-81 NHL season">1980–81</a>. Gretzky set new NHL records in <a href="/wiki/1980-81_NHL_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1980-81 NHL season">1980–81</a> for assists (109)<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and points (with 164).<sup id="cite_ref-points_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-points-16">[16]</a></sup> Also, they still had great draft positions. This allowed the Oilers to put together a young, talented, experienced team quickly. Within three years, Sather and chief scout <a href="/wiki/Barry_Fraser" title="Barry Fraser">Barry Fraser</a> had drafted several players who would have an important role in the team's success, including <a href="/wiki/Mark_Messier" title="Mark Messier">Mark Messier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Anderson" title="Glenn Anderson">Glenn Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jari_Kurri" title="Jari Kurri">Jari Kurri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Coffey" title="Paul Coffey">Paul Coffey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Lowe" title="Kevin Lowe">Kevin Lowe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grant_Fuhr" title="Grant Fuhr">Grant Fuhr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andy_Moog" title="Andy Moog">Andy Moog</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-draft_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-draft-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1981.E2.80.931983:_Learning_to_win">1981–1983: Learning to win</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1981–1983: Learning to win">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Oilers improved in <a href="/wiki/1981-82_NHL_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1981-82 NHL season">1981–82</a>, finishing second overall. <a href="/wiki/Grant_Fuhr" title="Grant Fuhr">Grant Fuhr</a> emerged as the Oilers' starting goaltender, and he set a rookie record by going undefeated in twenty-three straight games.<sup id="cite_ref-Fuhr_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuhr-18">[18]</a></sup> However, Gretzky stole the show by setting the single season record for <a href="/wiki/Goal_(ice_hockey)" title="Goal (ice hockey)">goals</a> with ninety-two<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and becoming the first player in NHL history to score 200 points (with 212).<sup id="cite_ref-points_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-points-16">[16]</a></sup> Gretzky's accomplishments helped the Oilers become the first team to score four hundred goals in a season, a feat they would accomplish for five straight years.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> However, the Oilers were upset by the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Kings" title="Los Angeles Kings">Los Angeles Kings</a> in five games (game three of this series, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Miracle_on_Manchester" title="Miracle on Manchester">Miracle on Manchester</a>, saw the Oilers take a 5–0 lead, only to lose 6–5 to the Kings in overtime).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1982-83_NHL_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1982-83 NHL season">1982–83</a>, the Oilers finished third overall in the NHL. They advanced all the way to the <a href="/wiki/1983_Stanley_Cup_Final" class="mw-redirect" title="1983 Stanley Cup Final">Stanley Cup Finals</a> (losing only once in the process) before getting swept by defending Stanley Cup Champions the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Islanders" title="New York Islanders">New York Islanders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1983_Edmonton_Oilers_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1983_Edmonton_Oilers-23">[23]</a></sup> During this season, Gretzky, Messier, Anderson, and Kurri all topped the 100 point plateau, with Coffey not far behind at 96.<sup id="cite_ref-1983_Edmonton_Oilers_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1983_Edmonton_Oilers-23">[23]</a></sup> After the season, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Fogolin" title="Lee Fogolin">Lee Fogolin</a> resigned as captain of the Oilers, picking Gretzky as his successor.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynasty_years_.281983.E2.80.931990.29">Dynasty years (1983–1990)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dynasty years (1983–1990)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1983%E2%80%9384_NHL_season" title="1983–84 NHL season">1983–84</a>, the Oilers finished first overall in the NHL, winning a franchise record fifty-seven games and earning 119 points (fifteen points ahead of the second place Islanders). They were the first team to feature three players with fifty goals (Gretzky, Kurri and Anderson).<sup id="cite_ref-Record_Breakers_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Record_Breakers-25">[25]</a></sup> Gretzky started off strong by scoring at least a point in the first fifty-one games of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-Gretzky_Record_Book_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gretzky_Record_Book-26">[26]</a></sup> Paul Coffey became the second defenceman ever to score forty goals in a season (with forty exactly).<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Oilers scored a grand total of 446 goals as a team, an NHL record.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The Oilers were so determined to win the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> that they hired <a href="/wiki/Roger_Neilson" title="Roger Neilson">Roger Neilson</a> as a video analyst.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> They started the <a href="/wiki/1984_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1984 Stanley Cup playoffs">playoffs</a> strongly by sweeping the Winnipeg Jets in the Smythe Division semifinals. They faced a tougher test in the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Flames" title="Calgary Flames">Calgary Flames</a>, but they defeated them in seven games in the division finals. They then swept the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_North_Stars" title="Minnesota North Stars">Minnesota North Stars</a> in the conference finals to earn a rematch with the Islanders in the <a href="/wiki/1984_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1984 Stanley Cup Finals">Stanley Cup Finals</a>. The Oilers split the first two games in Long Island, but then won three in a row in Edmonton to become the first former WHA team to win the Stanley Cup. After the series, Mark Messier was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Conn_Smythe_Trophy" title="Conn Smythe Trophy">Conn Smythe Trophy</a> as playoff MVP.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoilers_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoilers-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/1984%E2%80%9385_NHL_season" title="1984–85 NHL season">Next year</a>, the Oilers finished second overall in the NHL with 49 wins and 109 points. Wayne Gretzky led the NHL in goals with 73,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and Jari Kurri was close behind with a career high 71.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Gretzky also became the youngest player in NHL history to score one thousand points.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In the playoffs, the Oilers swept the Kings in the opening round and Jets in round two. They won the first two games of the Campbell Conference Finals against the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Blackhawks" title="Chicago Blackhawks">Chicago Blackhawks</a>, but lost the next two before winning the final two and returning to the Stanley Cup Finals. Edmonton lost the first game to Philadelphia, but won the next four to win the Stanley Cup for the second year in a row. Paul Coffey had a playoff performance to remember, setting records for most goals (twelve), assists (twenty-five), and points (thirty-seven) ever by a defenceman in a playoff year.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In addition, Jari Kurri tied <a href="/wiki/Reggie_Leach" title="Reggie Leach">Reggie Leach's</a> record for most goals in a playoff year, with 19.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, Gretzky won the Conn Smythe Trophy after setting the record for most points in a playoff year (forty-seven).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg/150px-Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg" width="150" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg/225px-Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg/300px-Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="621" data-file-height="915" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gretzky_statue_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wayne Gretzky statue outside <a href="/wiki/Rexall_Place" class="mw-redirect" title="Rexall Place">Rexall Place</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite some off-season legal issues,<sup id="cite_ref-Spoilers_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoilers-30">[30]</a></sup> the Oilers were again the top team in the NHL during the <a href="/wiki/1985%E2%80%9386_NHL_season" title="1985–86 NHL season">1985–86</a> regular season, with 56 wins and 119 points. They won the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Presidents%27_Trophy" title="Presidents' Trophy">Presidents' Trophy</a>, the trophy given to the team with the best regular-season record. Gretzky, Kurri, and Anderson each scored fifty goals again.<sup id="cite_ref-Record_Breakers_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Record_Breakers-25">[25]</a></sup> Kurri led the NHL in goals with 68, finishing with 131 points. Paul Coffey set a new record for most goals in a season by a defenceman (forty-eight), and he just missed setting a new record for points by a defenceman with 138 (<a href="/wiki/Bobby_Orr" title="Bobby Orr">Bobby Orr</a> scored 139 in <a href="/wiki/1970-71_NHL_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1970-71 NHL season">1970–71</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Gretzky also set records for assists (163) and points (215).<sup id="cite_ref-Gretzky_Record_Book_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gretzky_Record_Book-26">[26]</a></sup> However, the Oilers failed to win their third straight Stanley Cup, as the Calgary Flames defeated them in seven games in the second round of the playoffs. In the third period of a 2–2 tie during game seven, <a href="/wiki/James_Stephen_Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="James Stephen Smith">Steve Smith</a>, a rookie for the Oilers, accidentally sent the puck into his own net on his birthday. This goal stood as the game-and-series-winning goal.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/1986%E2%80%9387_NHL_season" title="1986–87 NHL season">1986–87</a> saw the Oilers capture their second straight President's Trophy with 50 wins and 106 points. Gretzky and Kurri were first and second in the NHL point scoring race, and Messier was fourth.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Edmonton returned to the Stanley Cup Final and faced the same opponent as they had in 1985, the Philadelphia Flyers. The Oilers took a 3 games to 1 lead in the series. However, strong goaltending by Flyers' rookie <a href="/wiki/Ron_Hextall" title="Ron Hextall">Ron Hextall</a> forced a game seven. The Oilers still prevailed by a score of 3–1. In the post-game celebration, Gretzky immediately passed the Stanley Cup to Steve Smith, now vindicated after his costly miscue the previous season.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> However, Hextall won the Conn Smythe Trophy.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Oilers began losing star players in <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NHL_season" title="1987–88 NHL season">1987–88</a>. Paul Coffey sat out the first twenty-one games of the season before getting traded to the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Andy Moog also failed to report; he was tired of being Grant Fuhr's backup goalie. Moog played for the <a href="/wiki/Canada_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Canada men's national ice hockey team">Canadian Olympic team</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1988_Winter_Olympics" title="1988 Winter Olympics">1988 Winter Olympics</a> before getting traded to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Bruins" title="Boston Bruins">Boston Bruins</a> for <a href="/wiki/Bill_Ranford" title="Bill Ranford">Bill Ranford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Despite the changes, the Oilers placed third overall in the NHL. Grant Fuhr started a league-record 75 games (this record has now been broken)<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and posted a team-record 40 wins.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In the first round of the playoffs, the Oilers dispatched the third place Winnipeg Jets in five games. The Oilers then defeated first-overall Calgary in a sweep. In the Campbell Conference Final against the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Red_Wings" title="Detroit Red Wings">Detroit Red Wings</a>, the Oilers prevailed in five games. The Oilers then swept the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Bruins" title="Boston Bruins">Boston Bruins</a> in five games in a best-of-seven series. This occurred because of trouble during game four. With the score tied 3–3 with 3:23 to play in the second period, a power outage hit the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Garden" title="Boston Garden">Boston Garden</a>, forcing cancellation of the entire game. The Oilers would win the next game (originally scheduled as game five) back in Edmonton 6–3 to complete the series sweep. However, all player statistics for the aborted game four in Boston are counted in the NHL record books. Gretzky won the Conn Smythe Trophy after leading the playoffs in scoring with forty-three points. After the Cup-clinching game, Gretzky implored his teammates, coaches, trainers, and others from the Oilers organization to join at centre ice for an impromptu team photo with the Stanley Cup. This started a tradition since continued by every subsequent Stanley Cup champion.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> After the season, Fuhr was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Vezina_Trophy" title="Vezina Trophy">Vezina Trophy</a> as the NHL's top goaltender.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1988.E2.80.931990:_After_Gretzky">1988–1990: After Gretzky</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1988–1990: After Gretzky">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In a surprising and shocking trade, Gretzky, along with enforcer <a href="/wiki/Marty_McSorley" title="Marty McSorley">Marty McSorley</a> and centre <a href="/wiki/Mike_Krushelnyski" title="Mike Krushelnyski">Mike Krushelnyski</a>, were traded to the Los Angeles Kings on August 9, 1988. In exchange, the Oilers received US$15 million, young star <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carson" title="Jimmy Carson">Jimmy Carson</a>, 1988 first-round draft choice <a href="/wiki/Martin_G%C3%A9linas" title="Martin Gélinas">Martin Gelinas</a> and the Kings' first round draft picks in 1989, 1991 and 1993. The trade occurred because Pocklington didn't want to risk Gretzky leaving Edmonton without getting anything in return. Gretzky had converted his personal services contract with Pocklington into a standard five-year player's contract with the Oilers in the summer of 1987 with an option to declare himself an unrestricted free agent after the 1988–89 season. During the 1987–88 season, Pocklington had approached Gretzky about renegotiating the contract, but Gretzky, unwilling to give up his chance at free agency, refused, which ultimately led to the trade. None of this was public knowledge at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the Oilers and their fans were still upset. <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Riis" title="Nelson Riis">Nelson Riis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_Democratic_Party_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="New Democratic Party (Canada)">New Democratic Party</a> leader in Canada's <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_Canada" title="House of Commons of Canada">House of Commons</a>, went so far as to ask the government to block the trade.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Several of the Oilers considered launching a team-wide strike, and even considered demanding that Pocklington sell the team.<sup id="cite_ref-p115_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p115-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The loss of Gretzky had an immediate impact in <a href="/wiki/1988%E2%80%9389_NHL_season" title="1988–89 NHL season">1988–89</a>, as the Oilers were only able to finish in third place in their division. Mark Messier was chosen to succeed Gretzky as captain.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Coincidentally, the Oilers' first round playoff opponent was Gretzky's Los Angeles Kings. Edmonton took a commanding 3–1 series lead, but Gretzky and the Kings fought back to win game seven 6–3 in Los Angeles. It was the first time since 1982 that the Oilers had been eliminated from the playoffs after only one round.</p>
<p>The Oilers underwent more changes during <a href="/wiki/1989%E2%80%9390_NHL_season" title="1989–90 NHL season">1989–90</a> season. <a href="/wiki/John_Muckler" title="John Muckler">John Muckler</a> replaced Glen Sather as head coach of the Oilers; Sather remained general manager and became the Oilers' president.<sup id="cite_ref-Muckler_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muckler-53">[53]</a></sup> During training camp, Grant Fuhr came down with a severe case of appendicitis. He missed the first ten games of the season, and when he returned he suffered a shoulder injury that eventually sidelined him for the remainder of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-Fuhr_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuhr-18">[18]</a></sup> This marked the emergence of Bill Ranford as a starter. Four games into the season, Jimmy Carson decided the pressure of playing in Edmonton was too intense, and he was traded to Detroit with <a href="/wiki/Kevin_McClelland" title="Kevin McClelland">Kevin McClelland</a> in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Petr_Kl%C3%ADma" title="Petr Klíma">Petr Klima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Graves" title="Adam Graves">Adam Graves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Murphy_(ice_hockey)" title="Joe Murphy (ice hockey)">Joe Murphy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Sharples" title="Jeff Sharples">Jeff Sharples</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The Oilers improved on their previous season, finishing with 38 wins and 90 points, good for fifth place overall in the NHL. Messier had 45 goals and 84 assists for 129 points, good for second in the NHL scoring race (behind only Gretzky).<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first round, the Oilers faced the Winnipeg Jets. Trailing the series 3–1 and trailing Game 5 by the identical score, the Oilers rallied to win the next three and take the series. In the division final, the Oilers met Los Angeles for the second-straight season. Edmonton swept the series 4–0, outscoring the Kings 22–10. The Oilers then met the Chicago Blackhawks in the Campbell Conference Final and fell behind 2–1 in the series. However, the Oilers won the next three games to earn a rematch of the 1988 Stanley Cup Finals with Boston. The Final will be remembered for Game 1, which still stands as the longest Stanley Cup Final game played in the modern NHL. Despite being soundly outshot by the Bruins, the Oilers won the game 3–2 when Petr Klima — benched for much of the game, and thus the only player on either team who not exhausted — scored at 15:13 of the third overtime period.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The Oilers would go on to defeat the Bruins in five games, and win their first Cup without Gretzky. For his superlative goaltending, Bill Ranford was awarded the Conn Smythe Trophy.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_in_success_.281990.E2.80.931996.29">Decline in success (1990–1996)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Decline in success (1990–1996)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Oilers lost another important player before the <a href="/wiki/1990%E2%80%9391_NHL_season" title="1990–91 NHL season">1990–91</a> season, as Jari Kurri chose to play the entire season with the <a href="/wiki/HC_Devils_Milano" title="HC Devils Milano">HC Milano Devils</a>. Grant Fuhr was also suspended for 60 games for drug abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The season itself was not a great one for the Oilers: they finished with 37 wins and 80 points, good for third place in the Smythe Division. In the playoffs, the Oilers met the Flames in the opening round. In a thrilling series, the Oilers won the series in seven games, led by seven goals by <a href="/wiki/Esa_Tikkanen" title="Esa Tikkanen">Esa Tikkanen</a>. Despite injuries suffered in the series with Calgary, they defeated the Los Angeles Kings in six games. Their success was unable to continue into the Conference Final, however, as they lost in five games to the Minnesota North Stars, who were making their <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(sports)" title="Cinderella (sports)">Cinderella run</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg/200px-Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg" width="200" height="84" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg/300px-Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg/400px-Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3316" data-file-height="1395" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rexall_Place_Edmonton_Alberta_Canada_01A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Northlands_Coliseum" title="Northlands Coliseum">Northlands Coliseum</a>, home of the Oilers from 1974 to 2016, was still an NHL arena at 41 years old.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The final star players from the Oilers left before the <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_NHL_season" title="1991–92 NHL season">1991–92</a> season. Grant Fuhr and Glenn Anderson were traded to Toronto,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Steve Smith was traded to Chicago,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and Jari Kurri was traded to Philadelphia.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Charlie Huddy was claimed by Minnesota in the expansion draft,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and Mark Messier was traded to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> a day after the season began.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The Oilers even lost their head coach, as <a href="/wiki/John_Muckler" title="John Muckler">John Muckler</a> left to become head coach and general manager of the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Sabres" title="Buffalo Sabres">Buffalo Sabres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Muckler_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muckler-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Green" title="Ted Green">Ted Green</a> replaced Muckler as head coach,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and Kevin Lowe succeeded Messier as captain.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the staggering amount of changes, the Oilers produced a comparable season to 1990–91, finishing third in the Smythe Division with 36 wins and 82 points. In the first round of the playoffs, the Oilers again met the Los Angeles Kings. Again, for the third time since the Gretzky trade, the Oilers defeated the Kings. In the next round, the Oilers defeated the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks" title="Vancouver Canucks">Vancouver Canucks</a> in six games to return to the Campbell Conference Final for the third straight season, this time facing the Chicago Blackhawks. However, the Oilers unexpected run in the playoffs came to a crashing halt, as the Blackhawks dominated every game and swept the series 4–0.</p>
<p>The departures of the stars from the 1980s exposed serious deficiencies in the Oilers' development system. The Oilers had done a poor job of drafting during the dynasty years,<sup id="cite_ref-draft_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-draft-17">[17]</a></sup> and the younger players hadn't had nearly enough time to develop before the core of the 1980s dynasty left town. This didn't become apparent for a few years; as mentioned above, the Oilers still had enough heft to make the conference finals two years in a row. However, it was obvious that they were nowhere near being the powerhouse that had dominated the league in the previous half-decade. In <a href="/wiki/1992%E2%80%9393_NHL_season" title="1992–93 NHL season">1992–93</a>, the Oilers missed the playoffs for the first time as an NHL team. They would not return to the post-season for four years, despite the emergence of young centremen <a href="/wiki/Doug_Weight" title="Doug Weight">Doug Weight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jason_Arnott" title="Jason Arnott">Jason Arnott</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_the_playoffs_.281996.E2.80.932004.29">Return to the playoffs (1996–2004)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Return to the playoffs (1996–2004)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1996%E2%80%9397_NHL_season" title="1996–97 NHL season">1996–97</a>, the Oilers made the playoffs for the first time in five years, thanks to stellar goaltending by <a href="/wiki/Curtis_Joseph" title="Curtis Joseph">Curtis Joseph</a>. In the first round, they upset the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Stars" title="Dallas Stars">Dallas Stars</a>, who had compiled the league's second best record, in a seven-game series. The Oilers won game seven on a goal by <a href="/wiki/Todd_Marchant" title="Todd Marchant">Todd Marchant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Overtime_(hockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Overtime (hockey)">overtime</a>. However, the Oilers surprise playoff run failed to continue, as the Colorado Avalanche defeated them in the next round.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EdmontonOilersRigger.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/EdmontonOilersRigger.svg/150px-EdmontonOilersRigger.svg.png" width="150" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/EdmontonOilersRigger.svg/225px-EdmontonOilersRigger.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/EdmontonOilersRigger.svg/300px-EdmontonOilersRigger.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="215" data-file-height="216" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EdmontonOilersRigger.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oilers "rigger" shoulder patch logo, 1996—2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1997-98_NHL_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1997-98 NHL season">1997–98</a>, Joseph led the Oilers to another first-round upset. After Colorado took a 3–1 series lead, the Oilers held them scoreless for eight straight periods en route to winning the series in seven games. Dallas and Edmonton met again in the second round, but this time, the Stars were the victors. The Oilers would make the playoffs in four of the next six years, but they were defeated after the first round every single time.</p>
<p>Despite their success over the past two seasons, the Oilers were in trouble off the ice. Owner Peter Pocklington had explored moving the Oilers to Minnesota during the 1990s. In 1998, Pocklington almost made a deal to sell the team to <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Alexander_(businessman)" title="Leslie Alexander (businessman)">Leslie Alexander</a>, the owner of the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Rockets" title="Houston Rockets">Houston Rockets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> (NBA) who would have moved the team to <a href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>. On March 14, 1998, hours before the deadline to keep the team in Edmonton, the <a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Investors_Group" title="Edmonton Investors Group">Edmonton Investors Group</a> agreed to pay $70 million to buy the club.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The EIG were spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Cal_Nichols" title="Cal Nichols">Cal Nichols</a>, who had a commitment to retain NHL hockey in Edmonton. The deal was finalized, on May 5.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and thus prevented them from being the third Canadian team to move in the 1990s (the Winnipeg Jets and Quebec Nordiques had moved earlier in the decade). The Oilers received support from the NHL for this very reason.<sup id="cite_ref-loonie_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loonie-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 22, 2003, the Oilers hosted the <a href="/wiki/2003_Heritage_Classic" title="2003 Heritage Classic">2003 Heritage Classic</a>, the first regular season outdoor hockey game in the NHL's history and part of the celebrations of the Oilers' 25th season in the NHL. The Oilers were defeated by the Montreal Canadiens 4–3 in front of more than 55,000 fans, an NHL attendance record, at <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Stadium_(Edmonton)" title="Commonwealth Stadium (Edmonton)">Commonwealth Stadium</a> in Edmonton. Unfortunately, the Oilers would fail to make the playoffs in the <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_NHL_season" title="2003–04 NHL season">2003–04</a> season.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-lockout_.282005.E2.80.932010.29">Post-lockout (2005–2010)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-lockout (2005–2010)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Oilers struggled with their small-market status for several years, but after the wiped-out 2004–05 season, they were aided by a <a href="/wiki/NHL_Collective_Bargaining_Agreement" title="NHL Collective Bargaining Agreement">Collective Bargaining Agreement</a> (CBA) between the NHL owners and players. This included a league-wide salary cap that forced all teams to essentially conform to a budget, as the Oilers had been doing for years.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> A more reasonable conversion rate of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">Canadian dollar</a> revenues to <a href="/wiki/U.S._dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. dollar">U.S. dollar</a> payroll in the new millennium also helped the Oilers to return to profitability.<sup id="cite_ref-loonie_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loonie-68">[68]</a></sup> Because of this, Edmonton was able to acquire <a href="/wiki/Chris_Pronger" title="Chris Pronger">Chris Pronger</a> (former winner of the <a href="/wiki/Hart_Memorial_Trophy" title="Hart Memorial Trophy">Hart</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Norris_Memorial_Trophy" title="James Norris Memorial Trophy">Norris</a> Trophies)<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Peca" title="Michael Peca">Michael Peca</a> (two-time <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Selke_Trophy" title="Frank J. Selke Trophy">Frank J. Selke Trophy</a> winner)<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> before the <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_NHL_season" title="2005–06 NHL season">2005–06 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peca_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peca-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2005.E2.80.932006_season">2005–2006 season</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2005–2006 season">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The team suffered from inconsistency during the first few months of the regular season, especially in goal and on offence. Goaltenders <a href="/wiki/Ty_Conklin" title="Ty Conklin">Ty Conklin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jussi_Markkanen" title="Jussi Markkanen">Jussi Markkanen</a> were unreliable in net, and Peca also struggled with offence.<sup id="cite_ref-Peca_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peca-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> However, in-season acquisitions, such as defenceman <a href="/wiki/Jaroslav_%C5%A0pa%C4%8Dek" title="Jaroslav Špaček">Jaroslav Spacek</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> defenceman <a href="/wiki/Dick_T%C3%A4rnstr%C3%B6m" title="Dick Tärnström">Dick Tarnstrom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> goaltender <a href="/wiki/Dwayne_Roloson" title="Dwayne Roloson">Dwayne Roloson</a> and left winger <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Samsonov" title="Sergei Samsonov">Sergei Samsonov</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> helped Edmonton finish the regular season with 95 points and clinch the final playoff spot in the Western Conference over Vancouver.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first round of the playoffs, the Oilers played the Detroit Red Wings (winners of the <a href="/wiki/Presidents%27_Trophy" title="Presidents' Trophy">Presidents' Trophy</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Despite Detroit's much better regular season record, the Oilers pulled off a six-game upset for their first playoff series win since 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-Oilers_season_results_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oilers_season_results-14">[14]</a></sup> Edmonton then met the <a href="/wiki/San_Jose_Sharks" title="San Jose Sharks">San Jose Sharks</a> in the Conference Semifinals. After trailing the series two games to none, the Oilers won the next four and became the first eighth-seeded team to reach a Conference Final since the NHL changed the playoff format in <a href="/wiki/1993%E2%80%9394_NHL_season" title="1993–94 NHL season">1994</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> There, the Oilers would beat the sixth-seeded <a href="/wiki/Anaheim_Ducks" title="Anaheim Ducks">Mighty Ducks of Anaheim</a> in five games, claiming the <a href="/wiki/Clarence_S._Campbell_Bowl" title="Clarence S. Campbell Bowl">Clarence S. Campbell Bowl</a> for a seventh time.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2006_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="2006 Stanley Cup Finals">Stanley Cup Final</a>, Edmonton met the <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Hurricanes" title="Carolina Hurricanes">Carolina Hurricanes</a>. During Game 1, the Oilers blew a 3–0 lead, lost Dwayne Roloson for the series, after he suffered a knee injury and ultimately lost 5–4 when Carolina's captain <a href="/wiki/Rod_Brind%27Amour" title="Rod Brind'Amour">Rod Brind'Amour</a> scored after backup Ty Conklin misplayed the puck. From that game forward, the Oilers used Jussi Markkanen in net.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> However, after trailing the series 2–0 and 3–1, the Oilers forced a seventh game with a 2–1 win in Game 3, a <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Pisani" title="Fernando Pisani">Fernando Pisani</a> short-handed overtime winner in Game 5 and a 4–0 shutout for Markkanen in Game 6. The Oilers, however, could not complete the comeback as the Hurricanes won Game 7 by a score of 3–1 to capture their first-ever Stanley Cup.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2006.E2.80.932010">2006–2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2006–2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 2006 off-season, many Oilers left the team. Four days after their loss to the Hurricanes, Chris Pronger surprisingly issued a trade request for personal reasons. Pronger was subsequently traded to the Anaheim Ducks in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Joffrey_Lupul" title="Joffrey Lupul">Joffrey Lupul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ladislav_%C5%A0m%C3%ADd" title="Ladislav Šmíd">Ladislav Smid</a> and three draft picks.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Several Oilers left via free agency and during the season, long-time Oiler <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Smyth" title="Ryan Smyth">Ryan Smyth</a> was traded to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Islanders" title="New York Islanders">New York Islanders</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ryan_O%27Marra" title="Ryan O'Marra">Ryan O'Marra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nilsson" title="Robert Nilsson">Robert Nilsson</a> and a first-round pick in the <a href="/wiki/2007_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2007 NHL Entry Draft">2007 NHL Entry Draft</a> (<a href="/wiki/Alex_Plante" title="Alex Plante">Alex Plante</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth_trade_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth_trade-86">[86]</a></sup> Not everyone left the team, however; the Oilers were able to re-sign Dwayne Roloson and Fernando Pisani. Having lost so many players, the Oilers posted a 32–43–7 record in <a href="/wiki/2006%E2%80%9307_NHL_season" title="2006–07 NHL season">2006–07</a>, their worst record since the 1995–96 season, and eventually finished in 11th place in the Western Conference. Throughout the season, the Oilers lost various players to injury and illness; at one point, they had 11 players out of the line-up and had to rely on emergency call-ups to fill their roster.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_NHL_season" title="2007–08 NHL season">2007–08</a>, the Oilers had a 16–21–4 record after the first half of the season. However, they improved the second half of the year and went 25–14–2 in 41 games for a final record of 41–35–6. Nonetheless, this was not enough to qualify for the playoffs, as the Oilers finished three points out in ninth place. During the season, <a href="/wiki/Daryl_Katz" title="Daryl Katz">Daryl Katz</a>, owner of the <a href="/wiki/Rexall" title="Rexall">Rexall pharmaceutical company</a>, purchased the Oilers from the Edmonton Investors Group.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Oilers announced a restructuring of their hockey operations on July 30, 2008, which saw the promotion of Kevin Lowe to the role of president of hockey operations and the hiring of <a href="/wiki/Steve_Tambellini" title="Steve Tambellini">Steve Tambellini</a> as their new general manager.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9309_NHL_season" title="2008–09 NHL season">2008–09 season</a> saw the Oilers finish with a record of 38–35–9. However, that was only good enough for 11th in the West. One bright spot during the season, however, was Oilers goaltender Dwayne Roloson, as he became the oldest goalie to play 60 NHL games in one season.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> After the season, the Oilers fired Head Coach <a href="/wiki/Craig_MacTavish" title="Craig MacTavish">Craig MacTavish</a> and hired <a href="/wiki/Pat_Quinn_(ice_hockey)" title="Pat Quinn (ice hockey)">Pat Quinn</a> as his replacement.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dustin_Penner.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Dustin_Penner.jpg/125px-Dustin_Penner.jpg" width="125" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Dustin_Penner.jpg/188px-Dustin_Penner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Dustin_Penner.jpg/250px-Dustin_Penner.jpg 2x" data-file-width="508" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dustin_Penner.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dustin_Penner" title="Dustin Penner">Dustin Penner</a> led the Oilers in points with sixty-three in 2009–10.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roloson left via free agency at the end of the season,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and the Oilers replaced him in goal with <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Khabibulin" title="Nikolai Khabibulin">Nikolai Khabibulin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The Oilers also worked out a trade with the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Senators" title="Ottawa Senators">Ottawa Senators</a> for star right wing <a href="/wiki/Dany_Heatley" title="Dany Heatley">Dany Heatley</a>, which would have seen <a href="/wiki/Dustin_Penner" title="Dustin Penner">Dustin Penner</a>, Ladislav Smid and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cogliano" title="Andrew Cogliano">Andrew Cogliano</a> go the other way, but Heatley refused a trade to Edmonton and was later acquired by San Jose.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Off-season moves failed to help the Oilers as they finished with the poorest record in the NHL in <a href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%9310_NHL_season" title="2009–10 NHL season">2009–10</a>. It was also easily their worst season as an NHL team.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Rebuild_.282010.E2.80.93present.29">The Rebuild (2010–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Rebuild (2010–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the season, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Renney" title="Tom Renney">Tom Renney</a> replaced Quinn as the Oilers head coach.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The one advantage to such a bad season was that the Oilers were able to make the first pick in the <a href="/wiki/2010_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2010 NHL Entry Draft">2010 NHL Entry Draft</a>. The Oilers selected two-time <a href="/wiki/Stafford_Smythe_Memorial_Trophy" title="Stafford Smythe Memorial Trophy">Stafford Smythe Memorial Trophy</a> winner <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Hall" title="Taylor Hall">Taylor Hall</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Spitfires" title="Windsor Spitfires">Windsor Spitfires</a> with their pick.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> They used the off-season to begin the rebuild of the club around their young talent.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Patrick_O%27Sullivan_(ice_hockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Patrick O'Sullivan (ice hockey)">Patrick O'Sullivan</a> was traded to Phoenix, in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Jim_Vandermeer" title="Jim Vandermeer">Jim Vandermeer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nilsson" title="Robert Nilsson">Robert Nilsson</a> was bought out of his contract and Oilers captain <a href="/wiki/Ethan_Moreau" title="Ethan Moreau">Ethan Moreau</a> was placed on waivers and claimed by the <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Blue_Jackets" title="Columbus Blue Jackets">Columbus Blue Jackets</a>. Along with these players, several others were allowed to enter free agency including <a href="/wiki/Mike_Comrie" title="Mike Comrie">Mike Comrie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine_Pouliot" title="Marc-Antoine Pouliot">Marc-Antoine Pouliot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Potulny" title="Ryan Potulny">Ryan Potulny</a>. Also during the off-season, radio announcer <a href="/wiki/Rod_Phillips" title="Rod Phillips">Rod Phillips</a> announced his retirement. Phillips had been the Oilers' play-by-play announcer since <a href="/wiki/1973-74_WHA_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1973-74 WHA season">1973–74</a>. Phillips would call ten specific games in <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_NHL_season" title="2010–11 NHL season">2010–11</a> before calling it quits.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_Edmonton_Oilers_season" title="2010–11 Edmonton Oilers season">2010–11</a> Oilers season would be documented in the series <i><a href="/wiki/Oil_Change_(TV_Series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil Change (TV Series)">Oil Change</a></i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png/150px-Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png" width="150" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png/225px-Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png/300px-Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png 2x" data-file-width="724" data-file-height="1068" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shawn_Horcoff_2010.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shawn_Horcoff" title="Shawn Horcoff">Shawn Horcoff</a>, 13th captain in Oilers NHL franchise history.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 2010–11 season would bring a new look to the Edmonton Oilers line-up, when <a href="/wiki/Shawn_Horcoff" title="Shawn Horcoff">Shawn Horcoff</a> was selected to succeed Ethan Moreau as team captain. Horcoff had become the Oilers longest-serving player by this point.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Taylor Hall, <a href="/wiki/Jordan_Eberle" title="Jordan Eberle">Jordan Eberle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnus_P%C3%A4%C3%A4j%C3%A4rvi" title="Magnus Pääjärvi">Magnus Paajarvi</a> would all make their NHL debuts for the team. Despite the influx of their young talent, Edmonton would still find themselves at the bottom of the standings. In an attempt to gain valuable prospects and draft picks, <a href="/wiki/Dustin_Penner" title="Dustin Penner">Dustin Penner</a> was traded from the Oilers to Los Angeles on February 28, 2011, in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Colten_Teubert" title="Colten Teubert">Colten Teubert</a>, a first-round draft pick in <a href="/wiki/2011_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2011 NHL Entry Draft">2011</a> (<a href="/wiki/Oscar_Klefbom" title="Oscar Klefbom">Oscar Klefbom</a>) and a conditional third-round pick in <a href="/wiki/2012_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2012 NHL Entry Draft">2012</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> At the end of the season, the Oilers were at the bottom of the standings and received the right to choose first overall in the upcoming <a href="/wiki/2011_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2011 NHL Entry Draft">2011 NHL Entry Draft</a>. The Oilers selected <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Nugent-Hopkins" title="Ryan Nugent-Hopkins">Ryan Nugent-Hopkins</a> with the first overall selection in the Draft, along with several other "blue chip" prospects. During the 2011 off-season, the team again made several moves to bolster the offence and defence, re-acquiring fan favourite Ryan Smyth from Los Angeles in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Colin_Fraser_(ice_hockey)" title="Colin Fraser (ice hockey)">Colin Fraser</a> and a seventh-round draft pick.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The team also traded with the Anaheim Ducks to acquire <a href="/wiki/Andy_Sutton" title="Andy Sutton">Andy Sutton</a> for <a href="/wiki/Kurtis_Foster" title="Kurtis Foster">Kurtis Foster</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sheldon_Souray" title="Sheldon Souray">Sheldon Souray</a>, who had played the entire 2010–11 season in the <a href="/wiki/American_Hockey_League" title="American Hockey League">American Hockey League</a> (AHL) with the <a href="/wiki/Hershey_Bears" title="Hershey Bears">Hershey Bears</a>, was bought out of the last year of his contract.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> These moves, coupled with the signings of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ric_B%C3%A9langer" title="Éric Bélanger">Eric Belanger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cam_Barker" title="Cam Barker">Cam Barker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Eager" title="Ben Eager">Ben Eager</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darcy_Hordichuk" title="Darcy Hordichuk">Darcy Hordichuk</a>, changed the complexion of the team, to add "grit and toughness." However, the Oilers were again unable to qualify for the playoffs for the sixth-straight season, as they finished 14th in the Western Conference.</p>
<p>On May 17, 2012, a month after the ending of the <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_NHL_season" title="2011–12 NHL season">2011–12 season</a>, the Oilers would announce they would not be renewing the contract of Head Coach Tom Renney.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The following month, Edmonton selected <a href="/wiki/Nail_Yakupov" title="Nail Yakupov">Nail Yakupov</a> as the first overall pick at the <a href="/wiki/2012_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2012 NHL Entry Draft">2012 NHL Entry Draft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> A week later, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Krueger" title="Ralph Krueger">Ralph Krueger</a> was named as the Oilers' new head coach on June 27, being promoted from his role as associate coach the season previous.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Three days later, Edmonton announced they had agreed to terms with sought-after free agent defenceman <a href="/wiki/Justin_Schultz" title="Justin Schultz">Justin Schultz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_NHL_season" title="2012–13 NHL season">2012–13 season</a> start, however, was delayed from its original October 11, 2012, date due to a <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_NHL_lockout" title="2012–13 NHL lockout">labour lockout</a> imposed by the NHL franchise owners after the expiration of the League's CBA. After a new labour agreement was reached between the owners and the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League_Players%27_Association" title="National Hockey League Players' Association">National Hockey League Players' Association</a> (NHLPA), training camps opened on January 13, 2013, and a 48-game season (reduced from 82 games) commenced on January 19. The Oilers would play their first game of the shortened season a day later, on January 20.</p>
<p>On January 23, to ensure of the health of the Edmonton Oilers in Edmonton and for the planned revitalization of downtown Edmonton, the City of Edmonton council voted 10–3 to the approval of a deal which will see a new $480 million arena built in Edmonton's downtown core for the start of the 2016–17 season. <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Communications" title="Rogers Communications">Rogers Communications</a> announced it would have the naming rights to the new arena on December 3, 2013; the new 18,641-seat arena is to be called <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Place" title="Rogers Place">Rogers Place</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>After 41 games into the shortened season, and with the Oilers mathematically eliminated from the playoffs for a seventh successive time, Edmonton dismissed General Manager Steve Tambellini, whereupon he was replaced with former Head Coach Craig MacTavish.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Following the end of the season, on June 8, MacTavish fired Ralph Krueger after just one season as head coach.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Two days later, it was announced that Krueger was to be replaced by former <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Marlies" title="Toronto Marlies">Toronto Marlies</a> head coach <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Eakins" title="Dallas Eakins">Dallas Eakins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Some of MacTavish's first player moves as Oilers GM came at the <a href="/wiki/2013_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2013 NHL Entry Draft">2013 NHL Entry Draft</a>, as Edmonton would use their seventh overall selection to draft defenceman <a href="/wiki/Darnell_Nurse" title="Darnell Nurse">Darnell Nurse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> More moves came on July 5, during free agency, which saw MacTavish trade away captain Shawn Horcoff to the Dallas Stars in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Philip_Larsen" title="Philip Larsen">Philip Larsen</a>. MacTavish also signed <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ference" title="Andrew Ference">Andrew Ference</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boyd_Gordon" title="Boyd Gordon">Boyd Gordon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jason_LaBarbera" title="Jason LaBarbera">Jason LaBarbera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Will_Acton" title="Will Acton">Will Acton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Hamilton" title="Ryan Hamilton">Ryan Hamilton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Joensuu" title="Jesse Joensuu">Jesse Joensuu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Ference was later announced as the 14th captain in Oilers NHL franchise history, on September 29.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ale%C5%A1_Hemsk%C3%BD" title="Aleš Hemský">Ales Hemsky</a> and Ryan Smyth, who after the Horcoff trade became the last remaining members of the Oilers' 2006 Stanley Cup finalists still with the team, departed the Oilers franchise, as Hemsky was traded to the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Senators" title="Ottawa Senators">Ottawa Senators</a>, on March 5, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Smyth (who had previously left the Oilers in 2007, but had returned in 2011) announced his retirement on April 11,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> playing his final NHL game on April 13, where he was ceremoniously named team captain.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg/150px-Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg" width="150" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg/225px-Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg/300px-Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2940" data-file-height="3230" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Ference_-_Edmonton_Oilers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ference" title="Andrew Ference">Andrew Ference</a>, 14th captain in Oilers NHL franchise history.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On December 15, 2014, after 31 games into the <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9315_NHL_season" title="2014–15 NHL season">2014–15 season</a>, MacTavish announced that Dallas Eakins was relieved of head coaching duties. MacTavish assumed the role of interim coach while <a href="/wiki/Todd_Nelson_(ice_hockey)" title="Todd Nelson (ice hockey)">Todd Nelson</a> transitioned into the role for the remainder of the season. Nelson was previously serving as the head coach of the <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City_Barons" title="Oklahoma City Barons">Oklahoma City Barons</a>, the Oilers' then-AHL affiliation team.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Three days later, the Oilers released a statement, which would see their affiliation with the Barons cease operations at the end of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following Edmonton's decision not to renew affiliation with the Barons, it was announced that the Oilers would be relocating their AHL franchise from <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City" title="Oklahoma City">Oklahoma City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Bakersfield,_California" title="Bakersfield, California">Bakersfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. This move was announced on January 29, 2015, as part of the AHL's new Pacific Division, which would include the Oilers affiliation in Bakersfield.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The following month, on February 25, the team was given its new identity, the <a href="/wiki/Bakersfield_Condors_(AHL)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bakersfield Condors (AHL)">Bakersfield Condors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> On April 2, the Condors released their new logo.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Oilers won the 2015 Draft Lottery that took place on April 18, thus moving them from the third overall pick to first overall, marking their fourth lottery win in six seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The Oilers used the pick to select <a href="/wiki/Connor_McDavid" title="Connor McDavid">Connor McDavid</a> first overall in the <a href="/wiki/2015_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2015 NHL Entry Draft">2015 NHL Entry Draft</a> held in Sunrise, Florida on June 26.</p>
<p>On April 24, Craig MacTavish was removed from his position as general manager and was replaced by former <a href="/wiki/Boston_Bruins" title="Boston Bruins">Boston Bruins</a> GM <a href="/wiki/Peter_Chiarelli_(ice_hockey)" title="Peter Chiarelli (ice hockey)">Peter Chiarelli</a>; Chiarelli was also appointed president of hockey operations as part of other related changes.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiarelli_Hiring_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiarelli_Hiring-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 19, <a href="/wiki/Todd_McLellan" title="Todd McLellan">Todd McLellan</a> was named the new head coach of the Oilers.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> He and his former team, the San Jose Sharks, mutually agreed to part ways on April 20, after failing to qualify for the <a href="/wiki/2015_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2015 Stanley Cup playoffs">2015 playoffs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> More coaching changes came on June 4, when it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Keith_Acton" title="Keith Acton">Keith Acton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Craig_Ramsay" title="Craig Ramsay">Craig Ramsay</a> had been relieved of their duties and would not be returning for the 2015–16 season.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In addition to these coaching changes, the Oilers also made some changes to their scouting staff on June 22, which saw both head amateur and pro scouts, Stu MacGregor and Morey Gare relieved of their duties. Amateur scouts Brad Davis and Kent Hawley, pro scout <a href="/wiki/Dave_Semenko" title="Dave Semenko">Dave Semenko</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Billy_Moores" title="Billy Moores">Billy Moores</a>, who served as Director of Coaching and Special Projects, were also relieved of their duties.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Season-by-season_record">Season-by-season record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edmonton_Oilers&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Season-by-season record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
